Player_Black (to Proxy):

The position suggests that Black has a strong structural and dynamic advantage. White's king is still uncastled in the center, and Black has the two bishops and well-centralized pieces. You also have doubled c-pawns (on c5 and c6), which is a common feature in certain Nimzo-Indian or QGD structures, usually arising after White exchanges a bishop for a knight on c6. 

The most principled and strategically sound approach here is to fix your pawn structure and open the position to exploit White's king.

By playing **...cxd4**, you achieve several positional goals simultaneously:
1. **Undoubling the Pawns:** You immediately eliminate your doubled pawns, leaving you with a rock-solid, unified pawn structure (a6, c6, d5, e6).
2. **Opening Lines:** If White recaptures with `exd4`, the e-file becomes half-open. Because White's king is still sitting on e1, you will be ideally placed to follow up with the central break `...e5!`. This will blast the center open, activate your light-squared bishop on c8, and create overwhelming problems for White.
3. **If White plays Nxd4:** Your response remains `...e5`, which gains a free tempo by attacking the knight and establishes a massive, dominating pawn center.

White's pieces are somewhat uncoordinated (the rook on a4 is misplaced), and opening the center now will capitalize on that before White has the chance to play `O-O`.

By playing **e5-e4**, you thrust your pawn forward, kicking the White Knight on f3. This move acts as a powerful wedge that splits White's coordination and seizes more space. 

Let's look at White's responses to `e5-e4`:
- The e4 pawn is rock solid, defended by both your d5 pawn and the f6 Knight. Therefore, White cannot capture it.
- The f3 Knight is directly attacked and lacks good forward squares:
  - `Nd4` fails because your c5 pawn covers the d4 square.
  - `Ne5` fails because your d6 Bishop and c7 Queen cover e5.
  - `Ng5` allows you to simply play `h7-h6`, forcing the Knight into a trap or a miserable retreat.
  - `Nh4` allows `g7-g5`, again trapping the piece since `Nf5` is covered by your light-squared Bishop on d7.
- Consequently, White is practically forced to retreat the Knight to its starting square (`Ng1`) or the passive `h2` square, crippling White's kingside development and handing you an overwhelming positional and strategic advantage.

**1. Identify White's Threat:**
Looking carefully at the board, your central pawn on `e4` is currently undefended. White's Queen on `e2` is staring directly at it down the open e-file (since `e3` is empty). None of your pieces (the `d5` Knight, `d6` or `d7` Bishops, etc.) are protecting it. **2. Evaluate Counter-attacks:**
- Pushing the pawn to attack the Bishop (`e4-e3`) seems tempting, but it ultimately loses the pawn for nothing after White replies with `Bxe3`. Even if you follow up with a check like `Bd6-b4+`, White's Bishop can simply fall back to `Bd2` to block, and you've given away your pawn.

**3. Choose the Best Defense:**

Playing `f5`:
- Firmly defends the `e4` pawn with another pawn, which is generally a golden rule in chess to free up your pieces for active duty.
- Gains more space on the kingside and establishes a rock-solid `e4-f5` pawn duo.
- Keeps your centralized "monster" Knight on `d5` right where it is.
- Doesn't create any weaknesses around your King since White lacks a light-squared Bishop to exploit the newly opened a2-g8 diagonal.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent opportunity has presented itself. By capturing the bishop on d2 with your pawn, you not only win a piece but also unleash a devastating double check against the White king.

Let's break down why this is the best move:
1. **Material Gain**: `e3xd2` WINS a full Bishop for a pawn. 
2. **Double Check**: By moving the e3 pawn, you open the e-file for your Rook on e8, giving a discovered check to the White King on e1. At the same time, the newly placed pawn on d2 also attacks the King directly!
3. **Forced Response**: A double check is the most forcing move in chess because it cannot be blocked and the checking pieces cannot both be captured. The White King is forced to move (to either d1 or take the pawn on d2).
4. **Initiative**: After the King moves, you keep the attack going, keeping White purely on the defensive.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent position for Black. You are up a significant amount of material (two bishops!) and your pieces are much better coordinated. Currently, your knight on d5 is under attack by the c4 pawn. However, you have a devastating forcing move that seizes the initiative entirely. 

By moving your dark-squared bishop to f4, you deliver a clean check to the exposed White King on d2. 

Let's break down why this is so powerful:
1. **The Check is Unblockable**: The diagonal from f4 to d2 passes through e3. White has absolutely no pieces or pawns that can safely step onto e3 to block the check. 
2. **Safe Square**: The f4 square is not attacked by any White piece.
3. **Forces the King into the Open**: Since the e-file is completely controlled by your rook on e8, the White King cannot step to e2 or e1. It is forced to move to d1, c2, or d3.
4. **Sets up Devastating Follow-ups**: No matter where the King steps, your attacked knight on d5 will simply jump to b4 with another tempo (either delivering another check or attacking a pinned piece), completely ripping apart White's position.

Action:

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the current position shows that your knight on d5 is under attack by White's pawn on c4. You must take action to prevent the loss of your knight. 

While you could retreat passively to squares like e7 or f6, you have a much stronger, forcing option: **d5b4+**. 

Here is why this is the best move:
1. **Safety with Tempo**: Moving the knight to b4 gets it out of the c4 pawn's attack while simultaneously delivering a check to the White king on c2. 
2. **Safe Square**: The b4 square is completely safe. No White pawn or piece can capture your knight there.
3. **King Disruption**: The check forces the White king to move to a less ideal square (such as c3, b3, d2, etc.). Moving the king to the center (like c3 or b3) exposes it further and blocks White's own pieces, while moving it to the back rank breaks its connection with the rooks.
4. **Follow-up Potential**: From b4, the knight remains active. If chased, it has access to excellent squares like c6, where it will attack White's undefended a5 pawn, or potentially d3.

Other active-looking moves like `Bxc1` (f4c1) or `Qxa5` (c7a5) would result in a net material loss after White responds by capturing your d5 knight (`c4xd5`). Moving the knight directly addresses the immediate threat while taking the initiative.

make_move d5b4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To decide on the best move, we need to understand the pawn structure and piece placement.

**Evaluation:**
The current position strongly resembles an Advance French or Caro-Kann pawn structure, where Black has a solid pawn chain in the center (d5, e4) against White's (d4, e3, c3). 
In such structures, Black's light-squared bishop (on d7) is traditionally a "bad" piece because it is heavily restricted by its own central pawns (d5, e4, f7). Trading off this bad bishop for White's "good" light-squared bishop (on e2) is a fundamental and highly desirable positional goal. 

**Candidate Move Analysis:**
Playing **d7-b5!** is a standard and highly effective maneuver in this exact scenario. 
Let's look at why:
1. **Trading the Bad Bishop:** You immediately challenge White's active e2-bishop. If White accepts the trade (`Bxb5`), you will recapture with your a6-pawn (`axb5`).
2. **Structural Benefits:** The recapture `axb5` would open up the a-file for your rook and place a pawn on b5, which clamps down on the queenside (controlling the c4 square) and gives you a space advantage. 
3. **Tactical Safety:** The b5 square is currently only attacked by White's e2-bishop, and perfectly defended by your a6-pawn. Furthermore, moving the bishop from d7 does not abandon any critical defensive duties (e6 is safely guarded by the f7-pawn). 
4. **Initiative:** If White refuses the trade and retreats the bishop, you've improved the scope of your worst piece to a much more active square with a single tempo. 

White's threats, such as the active Knight on g5, are currently well neutralized since f7 is protected and any ideas of Nxe6 just lose a piece to fxe6. 

Action: make_move d7b5

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the board shows that your light-squared bishop on e2 is currently under attack by White's Queen on c2. You must move the bishop to safety.

Looking at your retreat options, **Bc4** is strategically the strongest move. 
Here is a breakdown of why this is highly favorable:
1. **The c4 Outpost**: Your d5 pawn perfectly supports the c4 square. Since White has no b-pawn and their d-pawn has already committed to d4, the c4 square is an unassailable outpost for your piece. No white pawn can ever challenge your bishop there.
2. **Queenside Blockade**: Placing the bishop on c4 physically blockades White's c3 pawn. This permanently locks up down the c-file, effectively paralyzing White's queenside and heavily restricting the mobility of their c2 Queen and b2 Bishop. 
3. **Safe Retreat**: It evades the direct threat from the Queen while significantly improving your positional advantage in the locked-center structure (d5/e4 vs d4/e3).

The bishop will dominate the light squares from this post, crippling White's queenside counterplay.

**make_move b7b5**

I will play **b5b4**.

1. If White ignores it, we can play `...bxc3`. This will open the c-file for our c8 Rook and severely weaken White's d4 pawn, crumbling their center.

Since the c3 pawn is gone, our bishop on b4 will absolutely pin the White Queen on d2 to the King on e1, winning the game on the spot.

A strong and principled decision here is to capture the pawn on b4. 

White's queenside pawn structure is currently composed of pawns on c3 and b4 (with no a-pawn to anchor it). By playing **a5xb4**, you are forcing White to recapture.

If White responds with **cxb4**, it breaks the c3-d4 pawn chain. White's d4-pawn will be left with only the e3-pawn as a defender, giving you a long-term structural advantage. Additionally, this opens up the a-file for your c8 Rook.

This would immediately allow White's Knight to leap to e6 (**Nxe6**), executing a devastating fork on your Queen on d8 and Rook on f8! 

Breaking White's queenside structure while avoiding tactical pitfalls is the best way forward.

1. Moving the Rook off the c-file (e.g., `c6d6` or `c6e6`) will leave your other Rook on c3 completely undefended, allowing the White King on d2 to simply capture it. 
2. Retreating along the c-file (`c6c8` or `c6c7`) saves the Rook and keeps the c3 Rook defended, but it's a bit passive.
3. Moving to `c6c5` places the Rook diagonally in front of White's d4 pawn, which would immediately result in your Rook being captured (`d4xc5`). 
4. However, advancing the Rook to `c6c4` is a brilliant dual-purpose move. The c4 square is completely safe from any pawn attacks, and it remains on the c-file to perfectly defend the c3 Rook (which in turn defends your dangerously advanced d3 pawn). Furthermore, placing the Rook on c4 applies immediate attacking pressure on White's undefended d4 pawn.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent and safe choice here is to slide your rook down the c-file to c1. 

Currently, your knight on c5 is brilliantly placed in the center of the board, restricting the white king. The white king on d5 is currently adjacent to your knight and would love to capture it, but it cannot because your rook on c3 is defending it along the c-file. 

However, because the white king is so close, you have to be very careful not to stumble into a fork. For example, if you were to check the king with `c3d3+`, the white king could step to `c4`. Suddenly, the king would be attacking both your knight on c5 and your rook on d3. Since the rook would no longer be on the c-file to defend the knight, you would inevitably lose one of your pieces!

By simply sliding the rook back to c1 (`c3c1`), you maintain the unbreakable defense of your c5 knight while moving the rook completely out of the white king's reach. White has no pawn breaks and will be forced to harmlessly shuffle their king around while you retain a massive material advantage (a full rook and knight) to easily close out the game.

make_move c3c1

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent positional choice. Your Knight on c5 and Rook on c1 form a perfect "cage" that restricts the White King severely. Specifically, the Knight covers d3 and e4, the Rook controls the entire c-file (preventing the King from moving to c4 or c3 and protecting the Knight), and your f6 pawn covers e5. This leaves the White King with only two legal moves: d5 and e3. 

Moving your Rook off the c-file would hang the Knight to Kxc5, and checking the King with the Knight (e.g. c5e6+ or c5b3+) would break the cage and let the White King escape to squares like c4 or e4. 

Since White's pawns are completely paralyzed, White is essentially in a squeeze. The most principled endgame strategy here is to maintain this perfect restriction and march your King from h8 toward the center to assist in delivering checkmate.

make_move h8g8

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The current board state shows that you (Black) have a King on f7, Pawns on f6, g7, h5, a Knight on e4, and a Rook on e2. White has a King on f3, and Pawns on f5, h4. 

We can evaluate the available checks to force the White King:
1. `e2e3+` leaves your Rook unprotected, allowing `Kxe3`.
2. `e2f2+` leaves your Knight on e4 unprotected since the Rook moves off the e-file, allowing `Kxe4`.
3. `e4g5+` delivers check, but White's pawn on h4 covers the g5 square, meaning White can simply respond with `hxg5`, winning your Knight.
4. `e4d2+` is a safe Knight move. It delivers check to the White King on f3 (`d2` to `f3` is a valid Knight jump). The d2 square is completely out of reach from the White King and pawns.

After `e4d2+`, the White King is severely restricted. Your Rook on e2 cuts off the entire 2nd rank and controls the e-file (including the now-empty e4 square), while your h5 pawn covers g4. White's King will be forced to retreat to either f4 or g3, allowing you to sustain a strong attack and safely restrict White further.

The move `e4d2` is a safe, forcing, and highly advantageous maneuver.
 
Let's proceed with this move.
![Thought process complete]
make_move e4d2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board state, White's pawn on f5 is attacking your pawn on g6. If you ignore it, White could play `fxg6+` and create a very dangerous passed pawn.

You have two main pawn moves to handle this threat:
1. `g6g5` to bypass the pawn and attack White's h4 pawn.
2. `g6f5` to simply capture the threatening f5 pawn.

While both lines are winning for Black, `g6f5` is the most straightforward and secure path to victory. By capturing the pawn, you win material for free and completely defuse any counterplay White might try to stir up with passed pawns. You will be left with a 3-to-1 pawn majority (pawns on f6, f5, h5 versus White's sole pawn on h4), and your King is perfectly positioned to support them while White's King is too far away to stop your advance. 

make_move g6f5

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To systematically win this endgame, the most effective strategy is to use the Queen to trap the lone White King in a "box" and gradually shrink it by keeping the Queen a knight's move away from the King. 

Currently, the White King is on d5. By moving the Queen along the c-file to c3, we accomplish several things:
1. We keep the entire c-file cut off, preventing the White King from escaping to the queenside.
2. From c3, the Queen controls the d4 and e5 squares diagonally.
3. The Black King on f4 covers e4, and the Black Pawn on f6 covers e5.

With d4, e4, e5, and the entire c-file covered, the White King is completely denied any forward or horizontal movement. The King is forcefully pushed backward and only has two legal moves: **d6** or **e6**. 

Once the King steps back, we can continue to follow it at a knight's distance (e.g., if Kd6, then Qc4) to effortlessly walk the White King to the 8th rank for a quick checkmate.

make_move c1c3

An excellent position for Black! You have two deeply advanced pawns on d3 and f3 which are causing immense problems for White's position. 

The most crushing move here is to capture the pawn on e2 with your d3 pawn.

Here's why **d3xe2** is the best move:
1. **You win a pawn immediately.**
2. **You trap the White Bishop on f1.** After `d3xe2`, your new pawn on e2 attacks the f1 Bishop. The Bishop is completely paralyzed: 
   - It cannot capture on e2 (`Bxe2`) without being taken by your f3 pawn (`fxe2`), netting you a Bishop for a pawn.
   - It cannot escape to g2 because your remaining f3 pawn covers that square!
   - It cannot retreat to h3 because White's own pawn is blocking it.
   - It cannot move to d3 because the Bishop cannot jump over the pawn on e2.
3. If White ignores the threat, you simply play `exf1=Q` on the next turn, winning the Bishop and promoting to a Queen. 

Notice that if you had played the reverse (`f3xe2`), White's Bishop would have had the g2 square as a safe escape because the d3 pawn does not cover g2. By playing `d3xe2`, you keep the f3 pawn intact to perfectly blockade the Bishop's only escape route.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent positional choice here is retreating the bishop to a safe square, as your light-squared bishop is currently under attack by White's g4 pawn. 

Let's look at your options for moving the bishop on f5:
1. **f5xg4**: Sacrifices the bishop for a pawn, as White's f3 pawn defends it. Not worth it, especially since you are already up a clean extra piece (a knight).
2. **f5g6**: A blunder because White's advanced h5 pawn attacks the g6 square, so you would just lose the bishop.
3. **f5e6**: While it looks like a nice active central square, it allows a nasty tactical trick. White has an unimpeded d-pawn and can immediately strike in the center with `d2-d4`, forcing your dark-squared bishop on c5 to retreat. After that, White follows up with `d4-d5!`, which creates a pawn fork that attacks both your knight on c6 and your bishop on e6.
4. **f5c8**: Passive and undefines your piece completely.
5. **f5d7**: The safest and most solid square. It maintains control, keeps the bishop active enough on the queenside/center, avoids blocking your rooks entirely, and crucially dodges White's potential `d4-d5` fork trick.

Given your piece advantage, simplifying and preventing White from getting counterplay or tricks is the path to a smooth victory.

make_move f5d7

Capturing this pawn with your Knight (`f6g4`) is an incredibly strong move for several reasons:

4. **Permanent Outpost:** Because White's f-pawn and h-pawn are missing/advanced, if White ignores the Knight, it becomes an unkickable "octopus" deeply planted in White's territory.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

This is a highly closed and maneuvering position. White's setup is extremely passive, with their pieces crammed on the first two ranks and the king stuck on f1. The pawn structure heavily restricts White, particularly your e4 pawn, which is a massive thorn in White's position, paralyzing their development.

In such closed positions, direct attacks are often difficult, and the game revolves around piece improvement and maneuvering. Your knight on c6 currently has no targets—d4 and b4 are well-defended, and e5 is covered. A classic and very powerful maneuver in this type of pawn structure (similar to advanced French Defense lines) is to reroute the queenside knight to a more active square. 

By moving the knight to e7, you prepare to jump into f5. From f5, the knight will be an absolute monster, putting pressure on White's d4, g3, and h4 pawns, while eyeing key central squares. 

There's no rush to capture on d3 (`exd3`), as resolving the tension would only help White untangle their pieces (e.g., allowing their Bishop to recapture on d3 and freeing their position). We want to maintain the squeeze.

Action:

By capturing **e4xd3**, you achieve several goals:
1. **Open the e-file:** This clears the path for your Rook on e8, directly eyeing White's Bishop on e2 and the vulnerable King on f1.

- `d4e5` (Queen takes Knight): This wins a clean 3 points. The Knight on e5 is unprotected and capturing it centralizes your Queen even further, pinning White's g3 pawn to the King in the process.

It will easily be able to route back into the game via b3 or c2.

Right now, the passed black pawn on c3 is under attack from White's pawns on b2 and d2. If Black does not do anything with this pawn, White will simply capture it with `bxc3` or `dxc3`, restoring their pawn structure. 

To make the most of this advanced pawn, Black can capture one of the attacking pawns. The options are `cxb2` and `cxd2+`. 
Capturing on d2 (`c3d2`) is overwhelmingly the best move here for a few reasons:
1. **It comes with a check**: The pawn will land on d2, attacking the White King on e1. This forces White to immediately spend a turn dealing with the check.
2. **Simplification**: Since Black is already up a full piece (a knight), trading pieces benefits Black. If White decides to recapture the pawn with their Queen (`Qxd2`), Black will be perfectly positioned to force a Queen trade (`Qxd2+`), greatly simplifying the board and stamping out any counterplay White might have hoped for.
3. **Disrupts development**: If White recaptures with the Bishop (`Bxd2`), the d-file opens up for Black's Queen. If White recaptures with the King (`Kxd2`), they lose their castling rights and strand their king in the center.

Winning a central pawn with a check while forcing simplification is an ideal way to solidify your already winning material advantage. Let's execute this sequence. 

Move: `c3d2` (cxd2+)

White will be forced to recapture with their King (`Kxf1`), permanently losing their right to castle and granting you the positional advantage of the Bishop pair while keeping their King awkwardly stranded in the center.

Therefore, `g4h2` is tactically sound and by far your most crushing continuation.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The board reveals that White's Bishop on g5 is currently attacking your Queen on d8 along the g5-d8 diagonal. At the same time, your Knight on g4 is attacking White's Rook on f2. 

If you just move your Queen to safety (e.g., `d8d7`), White can easily save their Rook by moving it away, and the game continues. 

However, by playing the pawn move `f7f6`, you accomplish three critical things simultaneously:
1. You successfully block the g5-d8 diagonal, shielding your Queen from the Bishop's attack.
2. Your pawn on f6 now directly attacks the Bishop on g5.
3. Your Knight on g4 is still attacking the Rook on f2.

This creates a devastating double threat: White has two valuable pieces hanging (the Bishop on g5 and the Rook on f2). It's impossible for White to save both. 
- If White retreats the Bishop (e.g., to d2), you will simply play `Nxf2`, winning the exchange.
- If White tries to save the Rook (e.g., by moving it to f1), you will play `fxg5`, cleanly winning the Bishop. 
- If White plays `Bxf6` to eliminate the pawn, you can recapture with `gxf6` (since the f6 square is well-defended by your g7 pawn, your g4 Knight, your f8 Rook, and your Queen), still winning material.

This textbook defensive block combined with a powerful counter-attack forces a material advantage for Black.
 
Action: `make_move f7f6`
